<li><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Open_Source_Awards" title="New Zealand Open Source Awards">New Zealand Open Source Awards</a></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Procedures">Procedures</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Procedures"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Open_security" title="Open security">Open security</a>, application of open source philosophies to computer security</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Open_Source_Information_System" title="Open Source Information System">Open Source Information System</a>, the former name of an American <a href="/wiki/Unclassified" class="mw-redirect" title="Unclassified">unclassified</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">network</a> serving the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Intelligence_Community" title="United States Intelligence Community">U.S. intelligence community</a> with <a href="/wiki/Open-source_intelligence" title="Open-source intelligence">open-source intelligence</a>, since mid-2006 the content of OSIS is now known as <a href="/wiki/Intelink" title="Intelink">Intelink</a>-U while the network portion is known as <a href="/wiki/DNI-U" class="mw-redirect" title="DNI-U">DNI-U</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Open-source_intelligence" title="Open-source intelligence">Open-source intelligence</a>, an intelligence gathering discipline based on information collected from open sources (not to be confused with open-source artificial intelligence such as <a href="/wiki/Mycroft_(software)" title="Mycroft (software)">Mycroft (software)</a>).</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Society">Society</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Society"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The rise of open-source culture in the 20th century resulted from a growing tension between creative practices that involve require access to content that is often <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyrighted</a>, and restrictive intellectual property laws and policies governing access to copyrighted content. The two main ways in which intellectual property laws became more restrictive in the 20th century were extensions to the term of copyright (particularly in the United States) and penalties, such as those articulated in the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Millennium_Copyright_Act" title="Digital Millennium Copyright Act">Digital Millennium Copyright Act</a> (DMCA), placed on attempts to circumvent anti-piracy technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-copyright1998_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-copyright1998-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although artistic appropriation is often permitted under <a href="/wiki/Fair_use" title="Fair use">fair-use</a> doctrines, the complexity and ambiguity of these doctrines create an atmosphere of uncertainty among cultural practitioners. Also, the protective actions of copyright owners create what some call a "<a href="/wiki/Chilling_effect" title="Chilling effect">chilling effect</a>" among cultural practitioners.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The idea of an "open-source" culture runs parallel to "<a href="/wiki/Free_Culture_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Culture movement">Free Culture</a>", but is substantively different. <i>Free culture</i> is a term derived from the <a href="/wiki/Free_software_movement" title="Free software movement">free software movement</a>, and in contrast to that vision of culture, proponents of open-source culture (OSC) maintain that some intellectual property law needs to exist to protect cultural producers. Yet they propose a more nuanced position than corporations have traditionally sought. Instead of seeing intellectual property law as an expression of instrumental rules intended to uphold either natural rights or desirable outcomes, an argument for OSC takes into account diverse goods (as in "the Good life"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2023)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>) and ends.
</p><p>Sites such as <a href="/wiki/CcMixter" title="CcMixter">ccMixter</a> offer up free web space for anyone willing to license their work under a <a href="/wiki/Creative_Commons" title="Creative Commons">Creative Commons</a> license. The resulting cultural product is then available to download free (generally accessible) to anyone with an Internet connection.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Older, analog technologies such as the telephone or television have limitations on the kind of interaction users can have.
</p><p>Through various technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Peer-to-peer" title="Peer-to-peer">peer-to-peer</a> networks and <a href="/wiki/Blogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Blogs">blogs</a>, cultural producers can take advantage of vast <a href="/wiki/Social_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Social networks">social networks</a> to distribute their products. As opposed to traditional media distribution, redistributing digital media on the Internet can be virtually costless. Technologies such as <a href="/wiki/BitTorrent_(protocol)" class="mw-redirect" title="BitTorrent (protocol)">BitTorrent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gnutella" title="Gnutella">Gnutella</a> take advantage of various characteristics of the Internet protocol (<a href="/wiki/TCP/IP" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP/IP">TCP/IP</a>) in an attempt to totally decentralize file distribution.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software_movement" title="Open-source software movement">Open-source software movement</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Government">Government</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Government"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Open_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Open politics">Open politics</a> (sometimes known as <i>Open-source politics</i>) is a political process that uses Internet technologies such as blogs, email and polling to provide for a rapid feedback mechanism between political organizations and their supporters. There is also an alternative conception of the term <i>Open-source politics</i> which relates to the development of public policy under a set of rules and processes similar to the open-source software movement.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Open-source_governance" title="Open-source governance">Open-source governance</a> is similar to open-source politics, but it applies more to the democratic process and promotes the freedom of information.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Open-source_political_campaign" title="Open-source political campaign">Open-source political campaigns</a> refer specifically to political campaigns.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/South_Korean_government" class="mw-redirect" title="South Korean government">South Korean government</a> wants to increase its use of free and open-source software, to decrease its dependence on proprietary software solutions. It plans to make open standards a requirement, to allow the government to choose between multiple operating systems and web browsers. Korea's Ministry of Science, ICT &amp; Future Planning is also preparing ten pilots on using open-source software distributions.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ethics">Ethics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ethics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Open-source ethics is split into two strands:
</p>
<ul><li>Open-source ethics as an ethical school – Charles Ess and David Berry are researching whether ethics can learn anything from an open-source approach. Ess famously even defined the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Internet_Researchers" title="Association of Internet Researchers">AoIR</a> Research Guidelines as an example of open-source ethics.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Open-source ethics as a professional body of rules – This is based principally on the computer ethics school, studying the questions of ethics and professionalism in the computer industry in general and software development in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Religion">Religion</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Religion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Open-source_religion" title="Open-source religion">Open-source religion</a></div>
<p>Irish philosopher <a href="/wiki/Richard_Kearney" title="Richard Kearney">Richard Kearney</a> has used the term "open-source <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>" to refer to the way historical figures such as <a href="/wiki/Mohandas_Gandhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohandas Gandhi">Mohandas Gandhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a> worked upon this ancient tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Media_2">Media</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Media"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Open-source_journalism" title="Open-source journalism">Open-source journalism</a> formerly referred to the standard journalistic techniques of news gathering and fact checking, reflecting <i><a href="/wiki/Open-source_intelligence" title="Open-source intelligence">open-source intelligence</a></i>, a similar term used in military intelligence circles. Now, <i>open-source journalism</i> commonly refers to forms of innovative publishing of <a href="/wiki/Online_journalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Online journalism">online journalism</a>, rather than the sourcing of news stories by a professional journalist. In the 25 December 2006 issue of TIME magazine this is referred to as <a href="/wiki/User_generated_content" class="mw-redirect" title="User generated content">user created content</a> and listed alongside more traditional open-source projects such as <a href="/wiki/OpenSolaris" title="OpenSolaris">OpenSolaris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">Weblogs</a>, or blogs, are another significant platform for open-source culture. Blogs consist of periodic, reverse chronologically ordered posts, using a technology that makes webpages easily updatable with no understanding of design, code, or <a href="/wiki/File_transfer" title="File transfer">file transfer</a> required. While corporations, political campaigns and other formal institutions have begun using these tools to distribute information, many blogs are used by individuals for personal expression, political organizing, and socializing. Some, such as <a href="/wiki/LiveJournal" title="LiveJournal">LiveJournal</a> or <a href="/wiki/WordPress" title="WordPress">WordPress</a>, use open-source software that is open to the public and can be modified by users to fit their own tastes. Whether the code is open or not, this format represents a nimble tool for people to borrow and re-present culture; whereas traditional websites made the illegal reproduction of culture difficult to regulate, the mutability of blogs makes "open sourcing" even more uncontrollable since it allows a larger portion of the population to replicate material more quickly in the public sphere.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Messageboards" class="mw-redirect" title="Messageboards">Messageboards</a> are another platform for open-source culture. Messageboards (also known as discussion boards or forums), are places online where people with similar interests can congregate and post messages for the community to read and respond to. Messageboards sometimes have moderators who enforce community standards of etiquette such as banning <a href="/wiki/Spamming" title="Spamming">spammers</a>. Other common board features are private messages (where users can send messages to one another) as well as chat (a way to have a real time conversation online) and image uploading. Some messageboards use <a href="/wiki/PhpBB" title="PhpBB">phpBB</a>, which is a free open-source package. Where blogs are more about individual expression and tend to revolve around their authors, messageboards are about creating a conversation amongst its users where information can be shared freely and quickly. Messageboards are a way to remove intermediaries from everyday life—for instance, instead of relying on commercials and other forms of advertising, one can ask other users for frank reviews of a product, movie or CD. By removing the cultural middlemen, messageboards help speed the flow of information and exchange of ideas.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/OpenDocument" title="OpenDocument">OpenDocument</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Open_format" class="mw-redirect" title="Open format">open</a> <a href="/wiki/Document_file_format" title="Document file format">document file format</a> for saving and exchanging editable office documents such as text documents (including memos, reports, and books), <a href="/wiki/Spreadsheet" title="Spreadsheet">spreadsheets</a>, charts, and presentations. Organizations and individuals that store their data in an open format such as OpenDocument avoid being <a href="/wiki/Vendor_lock-in" title="Vendor lock-in">locked into</a> a single software vendor, leaving them free to switch software if their current vendor goes out of business, raises their prices, changes their software, or changes their <a href="/wiki/Software_license" title="Software license">licensing</a> terms to something less favorable.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Open-source_film" title="Open-source film">Open-source movie production</a> is either an open call system in which a changing crew and cast collaborate in movie production, a system in which the result is made available for re-use by others or in which exclusively open-source products are used in the production. The 2006 movie <a href="/wiki/Elephants_Dream" title="Elephants Dream">Elephants Dream</a> is said to be the "world's first open movie",<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> created entirely using open-source technology.
</p><p>An open-source documentary film has a production process allowing the open contributions of archival material <a href="/wiki/Footage" title="Footage">footage</a>, and other filmic elements, both in unedited and edited form, similar to crowdsourcing. By doing so, on-line contributors become part of the process of creating the film, helping to influence the editorial and visual material to be used in the documentary, as well as its thematic development. The first open-source documentary film is the non-profit <i><a href="/wiki/WBCN_and_the_American_Revolution" title="WBCN and the American Revolution">WBCN and the American Revolution</a></i>, which went into development in 2006, and will examine the role media played in the cultural, social and political changes from 1968 to 1974 through the story of radio station WBCN-FM in Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The film is being produced by Lichtenstein Creative Media and the non-profit Center for Independent Documentary. Open Source Cinema is a website to create Basement Tapes, a feature documentary about copyright in the digital age, co-produced by the National Film Board of Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Open-source_film" title="Open-source film">Open-source film-making</a> refers to a form of film-making that takes a method of idea formation from open-source software, but in this case the 'source' for a filmmaker is raw unedited footage rather than programming code. It can also refer to a method of film-making where the process of creation is 'open' i.e. a disparate group of contributors, at different times contribute to the final piece.
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open-IPTV&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Open-IPTV (page does not exist)">Open-IPTV</a> is <a href="/wiki/IPTV" class="mw-redirect" title="IPTV">IPTV</a> that is not limited to one recording studio, production studio, or cast. Open-IPTV uses the Internet or other means to pool efforts and resources together to create an online community that all contributes to a show.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Education">Education</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Education"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg/125px-Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="83" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg/188px-Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg/250px-Global_Open_Educational_Resources_Logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="567" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Open_Educational_Resources" class="mw-redirect" title="Open Educational Resources">Open Educational Resources</a> logo</figcaption></figure>
<p>Within the academic community, there is discussion about expanding what could be called the "intellectual commons" (analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Creative_Commons" title="Creative Commons">Creative Commons</a>). Proponents of this view have hailed the <a href="/wiki/OpenStax_CNX" title="OpenStax CNX">Connexions</a> Project at <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice University</a>, <a href="/wiki/MIT_OpenCourseWare" title="MIT OpenCourseWare">OpenCourseWare</a> project at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Thacker" title="Eugene Thacker">Eugene Thacker</a>'s article on "open-source DNA", the "Open Source Cultural Database", <a href="/wiki/Salman_Khan_(educator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Salman Khan (educator)">Salman Khan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Khan_Academy" title="Khan Academy">Khan Academy</a> and Wikipedia as examples of applying open source outside the realm of computer software.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Open-source_curriculum" title="Open-source curriculum">Open-source curricula</a> are instructional resources whose digital source can be freely used, distributed and modified. Another strand to the academic community is in the area of research. Many funded research projects produce software as part of their work. Due to the benefits of sharing software openly in scientific endeavours,<sup id="cite_ref-BesançonPeiffer-Smadja2020_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BesançonPeiffer-Smadja2020-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> there is an increasing interest in making the outputs of research projects available under an open-source license. In the UK the <a href="/wiki/Jisc" title="Jisc">Joint Information Systems Committee (JISC)</a> has developed a policy on open-source software. JISC also funds a development service called <a href="/wiki/OSS_Watch" title="OSS Watch">OSS Watch</a> which acts as an advisory service for higher and further education institutions wishing to use, contribute to and develop open-source software.
</p><p>On 30 March 2010, President Barack Obama signed the Health Care and Education Reconciliation Act, which included $2&#160;billion over four years to fund the <a href="/wiki/TAACCCT" title="TAACCCT">TAACCCT program</a>, which is described as "the largest OER (open education resources) initiative in the world and uniquely focused on creating curricula in partnership with industry for credentials in vocational industry sectors like manufacturing, health, energy, transportation, and IT".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Innovation_communities">Innovation communities</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Innovation communities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The principle of sharing pre-dates the open-source movement; for example, the free sharing of information has been institutionalized in the scientific enterprise since at least the 19th century. Open-source principles have always been part of the scientific community. The sociologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_K._Merton" title="Robert K. Merton">Robert K. Merton</a> described the four basic elements of the community—universalism (an international perspective), communalism (sharing information), objectivity (removing one's personal views from the scientific inquiry) and organized skepticism (requirements of proof and review) that describe the (idealised) scientific community.
</p><p>These principles are, in part, complemented by US law's focus on protecting expression and method but not the ideas themselves. There is also a tradition of publishing research results to the scientific community instead of keeping all such knowledge proprietary. One of the recent initiatives in scientific publishing has been <a href="/wiki/Open_access_(publishing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Open access (publishing)">open access</a>—the idea that research should be published in such a way that it is free and available to the public. There are currently many open access journals where the information is available free online, however most journals do charge a fee (either to users or libraries for access). The <a href="/wiki/Budapest_Open_Access_Initiative" title="Budapest Open Access Initiative">Budapest Open Access Initiative</a> is an international effort with the goal of making all research articles available free on the Internet.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a> has recently proposed a policy on "Enhanced Public Access to NIH Research Information". This policy would provide a free, searchable resource of NIH-funded results to the public and with other international repositories six months after its initial publication. The NIH's move is an important one because there is significant amount of public funding in scientific research. Many of the questions have yet to be answered—the balancing of profit vs. public access, and ensuring that desirable standards and incentives do not diminish with a shift to open access.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> was an early contributor eventually donating all his inventions including the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_stove" title="Franklin stove">Franklin stove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bifocals" title="Bifocals">bifocals</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning rod</a> to the public domain. New NGO communities are starting to use the open-source technology as a tool. One example is the Open Source Youth Network started in 2007 in Lisboa by ISCA members.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Open_innovation" title="Open innovation">Open innovation</a> is also a new emerging concept which advocate putting R&amp;D in a common pool. The <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_(software)" title="Eclipse (software)">Eclipse</a> platform is openly presenting itself as an Open innovation network.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Arts_and_recreation">Arts and recreation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Arts and recreation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Copyright protection is used in the performing arts and even in athletic activities. Some groups have attempted to remove copyright from such practices.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, Russian music composer, scientist and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Pirate_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Pirate Party">Russian Pirate Party</a> member <a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_Numbers_(%D0%BC%D1%83%D0%B7%D1%8B%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BF%D0%BF%D0%B0)" class="extiw" title="ru:Complex Numbers (музыкальная группа)">Victor Argonov</a> presented detailed raw files of his electronic opera "2032"<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> under free license <a href="/wiki/CC_BY-NC" class="mw-redirect" title="CC BY-NC">CC BY-NC 3.0</a> (later relicensed under <a href="/wiki/CC_BY-SA" class="mw-redirect" title="CC BY-SA">CC BY-SA 4.0</a><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>). This opera was originally composed and published in 2007 by Russian label <a href="/wiki/MC_Entertainment" title="MC Entertainment">MC Entertainment</a> as a commercial product, but then the author changed its status to free. In his blog<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> he said that he decided to open raw files (including wav, midi and other used formats) to the public to support worldwide pirate actions against <a href="/wiki/SOPA" class="mw-redirect" title="SOPA">SOPA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Personal_Information_Protection_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal Information Protection Act">PIPA</a>. Several Internet resources called "2032" the first open-source musical opera in history.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_related_movements">Other related movements</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Other related movements"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1251242444">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0.5em;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image{border:none;padding:2px 0 2px 0.5em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-imageright{border:none;padding:2px 0.5em 2px .ambox{margin:0 10%}}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .ambox{display:none!important}}</style><table class="box-Cleanup_split plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="17" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="60" data-file-height="20" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article or section <b>may need to be cleaned up or summarized</b> because it has been split from/to <i><a href="/wiki/Open-source_software_movement" title="Open-source software movement">Open-source software movement</a></i>.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Notable events and applications that have been developed via the <a href="/wiki/Open-source_community" class="mw-redirect" title="Open-source community">open source community</a>, and echo the ideologies of the open source movement,<sup id="cite_ref-ref_e_TnL_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref_e_TnL-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> include the <a href="/wiki/Open_Education_Consortium" class="mw-redirect" title="Open Education Consortium">Open Education Consortium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Project_Gutenberg" title="Project Gutenberg">Project Gutenberg</a>, Synthethic Biology, and Wikipedia. The Open Education Consortium is an organization composed of various colleges that support open source and share some of their material online. This organization, headed by <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a>, was established to aid in the exchange of open source educational materials. Wikipedia is a user-generated <a href="/wiki/Internet_encyclopedia_project" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet encyclopedia project">online encyclopedia</a> with sister projects in academic areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Wikiversity" title="Wikiversity">Wikiversity</a>—a community dedicated to the creation and exchange of learning materials.<sup id="cite_ref-ref_e_Warger_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref_e_Warger-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2016)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Prior to the existence of <a href="/wiki/Google_Scholar" title="Google Scholar">Google Scholar</a> Beta, Project Gutenberg was the first supplier of <a href="/wiki/E-book" class="mw-redirect" title="E-book">electronic books</a> and the first free library project.<sup id="cite_ref-ref_e_Warger_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref_e_Warger-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2016)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Synthetic Biology is a new technology that promises to enable cheap, lifesaving new drugs, as well as helping to yield biofuels that may help to solve our energy problem. Although synthetic biology has not yet come out of its lab stage, it has potential to become industrialized in the near future. To industrialize open source science, there are some scientists who are trying to build their own brand of it.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ideologically-related_movements">Ideologically-related movements</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Open_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Ideologically-related movements"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Open-access_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Open-access movement">open-access movement</a> is a movement that is similar in ideology to the open source movement. Members of this movement maintain that academic material should be readily available to provide help with "future research, assist in teaching and aid in academic purposes." The open-access movement aims to eliminate subscription fees and licensing restrictions of academic materials.<sup id="cite_ref-ref_a4_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref_a4-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Free-culture_movement" title="Free-culture movement">free-culture movement</a> is a movement that seeks to achieve a culture that engages in collective freedom via freedom of expression, free public access to knowledge and information, full demonstration of creativity and innovation in various arenas, and promotion of citizen liberties.<sup id="cite_ref-FreeCulture_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FreeCulture-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Creative_Commons" title="Creative Commons">Creative Commons</a> is an organization that "develops, supports, and stewards legal and technical infrastructure that maximizes digital creativity, sharing, and innovation." It encourages the use of protected properties online for research, education, and creative purposes in pursuit of a universal access. Creative Commons provides an infrastructure through a set of copyright licenses and tools that creates a better balance within the realm of "all rights reserved" properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Creative_Commons_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creative_Commons-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Creative Commons license offers a slightly more lenient alternative to "all rights reserved" copyrights for those who do not wish to exclude the use of their material.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Zeitgeist_Movement" title="The Zeitgeist Movement">The Zeitgeist Movement</a> (TZM) is an international social movement that advocates a transition into a <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable</a> "resource-based economy" based on <a href="/wiki/Collaboration" title="Collaboration">collaboration</a> in which monetary incentives are replaced by commons-based ones with everyone <a href="/wiki/Sharing_economy" title="Sharing economy">having access</a> to everything (from code to products) as in "open source everything".<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While its activism and events are typically focused on media and education, TZM is a major supporter of open source projects worldwide since they allow for uninhibited advancement of science and technology, independent of constraints posed by institutions of patenting and capitalist investment.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> P2P Foundation is an "international organization focused on studying, researching, documenting and promoting <a href="/wiki/Social_peer-to-peer_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="Social peer-to-peer processes">peer to peer practices</a> in a very broad sense." Its objectives incorporate those of the open source movement, whose principles are integrated in a larger socio-economic model.<sup id="cite_ref-P2P_Foundation_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P2P_Foundation-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Opensym-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Opensym_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFRiehle" class="citation web cs1">Riehle, Dirk. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130312135554/http://www.wikisym.org/2012/09/28/definition-of-open-collaboration/">"Definition of Open Collaboration"</a>. <i>The Joint International Symposium on Open Collaboration</i>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 March</span> 2013</span>. <q>Open collaboration is collaboration that is egalitarian (everyone can join, no principled or artificial barriers to participation exist), meritocratic (decisions and status are merit-based rather than imposed) and self-organizing (processes adapt to people rather than people adapt to pre-defined processes).</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Joint+International+Symposium+on+Open+Collaboration&amp;rft.atitle=Definition+of+Open+Collaboration&amp;rft.aulast=Riehle&amp;rft.aufirst=Dirk&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.wikisym.org%2F2012%2F09%2F28%2Fdefinition-of-open-collaboration%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOpen+source" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The concept expands upon a statement found in the <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Definition" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Software Definition">Free Software Definition</a>: "Free software is a matter of liberty, not price. To understand the concept, you should think of 'free' as in 'free speech' not as in 'free beer.'"</span>
